    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="ce682614687b4886adfd2c4b4172c4c0">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: 'aefedd8b-aae7-4dc1-bdba-fbcf6730f134'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

Conductivity for Ultra-fast Sodium-ion Storage link" href="https://doi.org/10.1002/adfm.202409295">https://doi.org/10.1002/adfm.202409295</a></div><div class="epub-section    <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d93046718" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Attracted by the high specific capacity and energy density, transition metal-sulfides exhibit great application potential as sodium-ion batteries anode, but still suffer from the uncontrolled separation of M/S phase and inferior conductivity. Herein, the flower-like Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S@Sb@C (FF) is rationally tailored, accompanied by double-layer heterojunctions and C–S–Sb/Sb–S–Fe “bridge” bonds. Meanwhile, the bimetallic phases/boundary defects and built-in electric fields are formed with a strong electronic coupling effect, effectively alleviating the separation of the M/S phase (Fe/Sb and S), and significantly enhancing ion/e<sup>−</sup> conductivity. As expected, FF delivers an ultra-fast sodium-ions storage rate capacity of 436.5/334.3 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> even at 10.0/20.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>. When the operation temperature is lowered to -5 °C, the reversible capacity can still remain at ≈349.7 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>. Assisted by the detailed kinetic analysis and theoretical calculations, their great ion-storage abilities mainly derive from improved interfacial Na<sup>+</sup>/e<sup>−</sup> transfer and surface/near-surface redox behaviors. Moreover, the reassembling evolution of active phases is revealed by in/ex situ techniques, further demonstrating the stable adsorption/anchoring of intermediate reaction products Fe/Sb–S phases on the heterointerface, accompanying high reversible conversion-alloying reaction. Given this, this interesting work is anticipated to offer an in-depth insight into capacity fading mechanism, and effective strategy to design metal-sulfur anodes for advanced sodium-ion systems.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202409295-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202409295-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Captured by the high specific capacity and abundant redox reaction sites, transition metal sulfides (TMSs) have attracted tremendous attention as promising LIBs/SIBs anode candidates.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202409295-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202409295-bib-0001_R_d93046700e419" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Adv. Mater. Adv. Mater. Adv. Energy Mater. Energy Storage Mater. Energy Storage Mater.">1</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, their ion-storage abilities were always limited by large volume expansion and the notorious “shuttling effect” of polysulfides (NaPSs).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202409295-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202409295-bib-0002_R_d93046700e424" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Nano Energy Adv. Energy Mater. Nano Res. Adv. Funct. Mater. J. Mater. Chem. A">2</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> For previous reports, the issues above have been regarded as the dominant factors of electrochemical performance fading, while the in-depth reasons were scarcely revealed.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202409295-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202409295-bib-0003_R_d93046700e429" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Adv. Funct. Mater. Small ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">3</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In fact, for TMSs, the serious capacity fading mainly resulted from the separation of two single phases (metal section and sulfur region).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202409295-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202409295-bib-0004_R_d93046700e435" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Chem. Mater. Adv. Funct. Mater. Energy Storage Mater. Adv. Mater. Interfaces">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The aggregation and crystallization of two fresh phases should be noted, obviously leading to serious volume expansions and polysulfide dissolution/shuttling. Meanwhile, owing to the high surface free energy and electrochemical stresses, their separation tendency could be accelerated, further injuring the integrity of the structure and reversibility of the conversion reaction.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202409295-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202409295-bib-0005_R_d93046700e440" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Chemistry Adv. Funct. Mater.">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> That is to say, the recrystallization and internal stresses derived from the conversion reaction could induce severe coarsening of the intermediate product's metal-phase nanograin, significantly reducing the effective reaction interface between metal-phase and sulfur-phase (Na<sub>2</sub>S), finally resulting in an incomplete transformation of metal-cation/anion phases back to the original TMSs during desodiation process.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202409295-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202409295-bib-0006_R_d93046700e448" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Adv. Mater. Energy Environ. Sci. Energy Environ. Sci. Adv. Energy Mater. Adv. Funct. Mater.">6</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Moreover, on account of the poor intrinsic electrical conductivity, the slow electron transfer kinetics have been regarded as another issue for its attenuation of electrochemical properties.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202409295-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202409295-bib-0007_R_d93046700e453" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Nano-Micro Lett. Nano Energy Solid State Commun. J. Power Sources ACS Appl. Energy Mater.">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Hence, the controlling of two-phase separation, improvement of electrical conductivity, and enhancement of conversion-alloying reaction kinetics/reversibility were necessary for the further development of TMSs.</p>
         
         <p>From previous reports, plenty of efforts have been devoted to solving these issues above, containing nanostructure designing and carbon recombination/coating.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202409295-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202409295-bib-0008_R_d93046700e461" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - J. Colloid Interface Sci. ACS Nano Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Adv. Energy Mater. Rare Met.">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Benefitting from their synergistic effect, the volume swelling could be alleviated well, accompanied by the improvement of electrical conductivity, finally leading to excellent reversibility and cycle stability.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202409295-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202409295-bib-0002_R_d93046700e466" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Nano Energy Adv. Energy Mater. Nano Res. Adv. Funct. Mater. J. Mater. Chem. A">2</a>, <a href="#adfm202409295-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202409295-bib-0009_R_d93046700e466" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Small J. Mater. Chem. A Adv. Funct. Mater. Adv. Funct. Mater. Ceram. Int. Adv. Energy Mater.">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> For instance, with effective nanostructure engineering and in situ chemical transformation, the Fe<sub>1−x</sub>S@CNTs with a 3D network were successfully prepared. The unique structure could bring about a considerable Na-ion storage capacity of 449.2&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.5 A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> after 200 loops.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202409295-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202409295-bib-0010_R_d93046700e478" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - ACS Energy Lett.">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, the two-phase separation still existed and remained to be explored, which was mainly attributed to the weak confinement and binding of the carbon matrix. That is to say, the macroscopic volume expansion could be inhibited by traditional carbon incorporating, but the in-depth issues have been not completely solved. According to previous studies, the introduction of binary metals and reasonable distance regulating of metal-cation/anion phases was expected to strengthen the tight contacting between metal/sulfur (M/S) phases and inhibit the two-phase separation, thus realizing the excellent trapping effect of metal phases toward sulfur phase.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202409295-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202409295-bib-0003_R_d93046700e483" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Adv. Funct. Mater. Small ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">3</a>, <a href="#adfm202409295-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202409295-bib-0004_R_d93046700e483" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Chem. Mater. Adv. Funct. Mater. Energy Storage Mater. Adv. Mater. Interfaces">4</a>, <a href="#adfm202409295-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202409295-bib-0006_R_d93046700e483" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Adv. Mater. Energy Environ. Sci. Energy Environ. Sci. Adv. Energy Mater. Adv. Funct. Mater.">6</a>, <a href="#adfm202409295-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202409295-bib-0011_R_d93046700e483" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - ACS Nano">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>Interestingly, profiting from the competitive presence of binary metals, the adsorption/anchoring for sulfur phase and interface reaction reversibility could be effectively enhanced, fundamentally inhibiting large volume expansion and polysulfide shuttling.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202409295-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202409295-bib-0012_R_d93046700e490" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Chem. Eng. J. Energy Storage Mater.">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> For instance, utilizing CoZn-MOFs as precursors, Co<sub>1</sub>Zn<sub>1</sub>-S(600) anodes with abundant phase boundaries were prepared, exhibiting a high-rate capacity of 258.6&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at 10.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>. Benefiting from the designing of a unique heterojunction structure, their Na<sup>+</sup>/e<sup>−</sup> diffusion ability and reaction reversibility could be significantly improved, displaying excellent electrochemical reaction kinetics and capacitive behaviors.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202409295-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202409295-bib-0013_R_d93046700e508" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Adv. Energy Mater.">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Thus, constructing bimetallic sulfide composites with hierarchical heterojunctions has been carried out, which was beneficial for controlling two-phase separation and enhancing electrical conductivity.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202409295-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202409295-bib-0014_R_d93046700e514" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Small">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Excitingly, heterostructured Sb<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>@FeS<sub>2</sub> hollow nanorods, SnS<sub>2</sub>/Mn<sub>2</sub>SnS<sub>4</sub>/carbon NBs, and (SnCo)S<sub>2</sub>/SG nanocubes have been reported, effectively mitigating two active phases (metal Sb<sup>0</sup>/Sn<sup>0</sup> and Na<sub>2</sub>S) separation and nanosized Sb<sup>0</sup>/Sn<sup>0</sup> phases coarsening during the continuous sodiation–desodiation process. And the intermediate products of Sb<sup>0</sup>/Sn<sup>0</sup> and sulfide phases Na<sub>2</sub>S produced at deep sodiation state were effectively adsorbed/pinned through polymetallic phases (introduction of Fe/Mn/Co additive), thus inducing superior conversion/alloying reaction kinetics and reversibility of Sb<sup>0</sup>/Sn<sup>0</sup> and Na<sub>2</sub>S.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202409295-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202409295-bib-0006_R_d93046700e558" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Adv. Mater. Energy Environ. Sci. Energy Environ. Sci. Adv. Energy Mater. Adv. Funct. Mater.">6</a>, <a href="#adfm202409295-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202409295-bib-0011_R_d93046700e558" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - ACS Nano">11</a>, <a href="#adfm202409295-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202409295-bib-0015_R_d93046700e558" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - ACS Nano">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Moreover, the polymetallic heterointerfaces of bimetallic sulfides could also induce internal electric field and rich active storage sites, significantly shortening electron/ion diffusion paths and lowering conversion-alloying reaction energy barriers, thus resulting in excellent Na<sup>+</sup>/e<sup>−</sup> transport kinetics and surface/near-surface capacitive behaviors.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202409295-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202409295-bib-0016_R_d93046700e568" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Energy Storage Mater. Adv. Funct. Mater. Nano-Micro Lett. Sci. China: Chem. ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces J. Mater. Chem. A Energy Storage Mater. Energy Storage Mater.">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>Herein, assisted by vacuum calcination and gasification in situ coating manners, the self-assembled flower-like Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S@Sb@C nanosheets with double-layers heterojunctions (Fe/Sb and Sb/C heterojunction) were successfully prepared with purified natural stibnite (Sb<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>) and ferrocene. Supported by the synergistic effects of structural evolution, the bimetallic phases/boundary defects, interfacial C–S–Sb/Sb–S–Fe “bridge” bonds, and built-in electric field were formed with the strong electronic coupling effect and rich active storage sites, facilitating fast Na-ion/charge transport and S phase preferential adsorption/anchoring. Interestingly, it was in favor of alleviating the separation of M/S phase (Fe/Sb and S), Fe/Sb nanoparticles coarsening, and soluble NaPSs shuttling, finally bringing about remarkable ion/e<sup>−</sup> diffusion kinetics and conversion-alloying reaction reversibility. As SIBs anodes, FF delivered stable Na-storage capacity of 397.4 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 100 cycles at 5.0 A g<sup>−1</sup> and ultra-fast rate ability of 436.5/334.3 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 10.0/20.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>. Even at low temperature of −5 °C, the reversible capacity was still maintained at 349.7 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup>. Given this, this work was expected to shed light on significant insights about double-layers heterojunctions in preventing two-phase separation and strengthening electrical conductivity.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202409295-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202409295-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202409295-sec-0030">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202409295-sec-0030-title">2.1 Theoretical Calculations on Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S@Sb@C Double-layers Heterostructure</h3>
            
            <p>To further get insight into the effects of double-layers heterojunctions on enhancing electrochemical reaction kinetics and reversibility. First-principles density functional theory (DFT) calculations were carried out to provide atomic-level verification for the interfacial Na<sup>+</sup>/e<sup>−</sup> transfer behaviors and superior Na storage mechanism in FF. Based on previous studies, the band gap (<i>E</i><sub>g</sub>) of the material is closely related to its electrical conductivity, so the density of states (DOS) of FP, FD, and FF are shown in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202409295-fig-0001">1a–c</a>. All samples presented a continuous energy distribution across the Fermi energy level without obvious Eg, suggesting the metallic character.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202409295-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202409295-bib-0016_R_d93046700e626" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Energy Storage Mater. Adv. Funct. Mater. Nano-Micro Lett. Sci. China: Chem. ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces J. Mater. Chem. A Energy Storage Mater. Energy Storage Mater.">16</a>, <a href="#adfm202409295-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202409295-bib-0017_R_d93046700e626" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Energy Storage Mater.">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> That is to say, the bimetallic phase introduction and subsequent vacuum calcination were favorable for decreasing Eg and enhancing the conductivity of TMSs. Meanwhile, compared to FP and FD, the highest DOS around the Fermi energy level for FF was noted in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202409295-fig-0001">1d</a>, corresponding to its ultrahigh electrical conductivity. It was mainly attributed to the Fe/Sb/C double-layers heterojunctions and interfacial C–S–Sb/Sb–S–Fe “bridge” bonds engineering, effectively regulating the energy band/electronic structure and inducing more electron excitation/transition from valence to conduction band. In addition, from the charge density difference of FF as displayed in Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> (Supporting Information), it could be found that the electrons tend to transfer from C/Sb<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> to Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S, resulting in positive charges enriching on the C/Sb<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> layers, while the negative charges accumulated at Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S side.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202409295-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202409295-bib-0016_R_d93046700e650" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Energy Storage Mater. Adv. Funct. Mater. Nano-Micro Lett. Sci. China: Chem. ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces J. Mater. Chem. A Energy Storage Mater. Energy Storage Mater.">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, a built-in electric field was formed pointing toward Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S layer, remarkably accelerating ion/charge transport kinetics, thus guaranteeing high electrical conductivity and ultra-Fast Na storage. To further reveal the impact of heterojunction on electrochemical reaction kinetics/reversibility, the adsorption energies of as-resulted samples toward Na<sup>+</sup>/Na<sub>2</sub>S and corresponding adsorption structural models were calculated in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202409295-fig-0001">1e,f</a>. The Na<sup>+</sup> adsorption energy of FF was −3.30&nbsp;eV, obviously lower than that of FP (−2.34&nbsp;eV) and FD (−0.60&nbsp;eV), implying a stronger binding interaction and affinity. That is, the Fe/Sb/C heterojunctions with abundant active reaction sites and boundary defects could significantly boost Na<sup>+</sup> adsorption/transfer, resulting in considerable reaction kinetics/storage capacity. Moreover, the adsorption energies for Na<sub>2</sub>S were in order of FF (−4.87&nbsp;eV) &lt; FD (−0.83&nbsp;eV) &lt; FF (−0.48&nbsp;eV), further indicating that bimetallic phase introduction and heterojunctions/interfacial “bridge” bonds construction were beneficial for the preferential adsorption/anchoring and reversible transformation of discharge products. In a word, thanks to bimetallic phases in situ assembly, the double-layers heterojunctions and built-in electric field were constructed, accompanied by a strong electronic coupling effect and rich Na<sup>+</sup> storage sites (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202409295-fig-0001">1g</a>; Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information). It was expected to facilitate ion/charge fast transfer/diffusion, discharge products tight adsorption/anchoring, and reaction kinetics/reversibility enhancement, thereby effectively solving poor electrical conductivity and phase separation issues, finally bringing about superior cycling stability and rate property.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202409295-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/63f7d177-312e-48dd-a9a9-2642ab74cdc5/adfm202409295-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/63f7d177-312e-48dd-a9a9-2642ab74cdc5/adfm202409295-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/a168ae00-d922-485c-a681-ad1f157b7b22/adfm202409295-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/63f7d177-312e-48dd-a9a9-2642ab74cdc5/adfm202409295-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">

            </section>
         </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202409295-sec-0040">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202409295-sec-0040-title">2.2 Exploring the Physicochemical Properties of As-Obtained Samples</h3>
            
            <p>Flower-like Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S@Sb@C nanosheets with double-layers heterojunctions (Fe/Sb and Sb/C heterojunction) were successfully tailored to achieve remarkable conductivity and ultra-fast Na-storage capability as displayed in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202409295-fig-0002">2a</a>. The bimetallic phases/boundary defects, interfacial C–S–Sb/Sb–S–Fe “bridge” bonds, and built-in electric field were expected to strengthen the electronic coupling effect, active sites distribution, and M/S phases controlling/adsorption, thus inducing speedy ion/e<sup>−</sup> transfer and high-reversible conversion-alloying reaction. That is, this rational structural evolution was in favor of inhibiting the two-phase separation, Fe/Sb nanoparticles coarsening, and soluble NaPSs shuttling, finally leading to the considerable sodiation–desodiation reaction kinetics. Attracted by these advantages, the detailed exploration of physical-chemical properties was conducted for as-obtained samples. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202409295-fig-0002">2b</a>, X-Ray diffraction (XRD) characteristic peaks of samples accorded well with those of the standard pattern for hexagonal Sb (R-3m (166), JCPDs#85-1322).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202409295-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202409295-bib-0018_R_d93046700e729" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - J. Mater. Chem. A ACS Nano Small">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In addition, the weaker peaks located at 29.9°, 31.2°, 33.8°<sup>,</sup> and 43.7° were well indexed to (200), (204), (207) and (2014) planes of standard Pyrrhotite-7T Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S (JCPDs#20-0534), indicating the successful preparation of Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S@Sb@C (FF) composites.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202409295-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202409295-bib-0019_R_d93046700e741" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Mater. Lett.">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Interestingly, the higher peak intensity assigned to Sb of FF was noted, implying its high crystallinity and strong metallicity, which may be ascribed to the redox reaction of Sb<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> and the Fe-based precursors with low-boiling point during vacuum gasification. Moreover, the relative XRD patterns about Fe<sub>x</sub>Sb<sub>y</sub>S<sub>z</sub>@C (FD) and FeS<sub>2</sub>@Sb (FP) were displayed in Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3a</a> (Supporting Information). For FP, the peaks at 28.5°, 33.0°, 37.0°, 40.8°, 47.4°<sup>,</sup> and 56.3 ° corresponded to the (110), (200), (210), (211), (220), and (311) crystal faces, respectively, matching well with the standard patterns of Pyrite FeS<sub>2</sub> (PDF#71-2219).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202409295-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202409295-bib-0020_R_d93046700e767" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - J. Mater. Sci.">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Notably, no characteristic peak for Sb was observed in FP, perhaps derived from the low crystallinity and content of metallic Sb. From previous studies, the relatively low content of the metallic phase was not beneficial for improving electrical conductivity and capturing/anchoring the sulfur phase.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202409295-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202409295-bib-0006_R_d93046700e772" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Adv. Mater. Energy Environ. Sci. Energy Environ. Sci. Adv. Energy Mater. Adv. Funct. Mater.">6</a>, <a href="#adfm202409295-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202409295-bib-0016_R_d93046700e772" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Energy Storage Mater. Adv. Funct. Mater. Nano-Micro Lett. Sci. China: Chem. ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces J. Mater. Chem. A Energy Storage Mater. Energy Storage Mater.">16</a>, <a href="#adfm202409295-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202409295-bib-0021_R_d93046700e772" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Chem. Eng. J. Small">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> While all the diffraction peaks of FD were well associated with Orthorhombic FeSb<sub>2</sub>S<sub>4</sub> (JCPDs#83-0579). Meanwhile, for precursor Iron(III) acetylacetonate, the rich oxygen functional groups would preferentially participate in their in situ carbonization and sulfurization process, competitively plundering sulfur phase and carbon matrix (the production of CO<sub>2</sub>, SO<sub>2,</sub> and so on), finally resulting in the difference of lattice structure. Excitingly, the definite bimetallic phases/structure of as-prepared samples were anticipated to promote ion/e<sup>−</sup> diffusion and S phase adsorption/anchoring, thus enhancing its electrochemical reaction kinetics/reversibility. Further exploring their crystalline structure, the Raman spectrum of FP, FD, and FF were shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202409295-fig-0002">2c</a>, the peak at 147.8 cm<sup>−1</sup> and the peaks ranging from 250.0 to 450.0&nbsp;cm <sup>−1</sup> belonged to Sb–S and Fe–S bonds, respectively, confirming the formation of bimetallic sulfides.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202409295-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202409295-bib-0007_R_d93046700e796" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Nano-Micro Lett. Nano Energy Solid State Commun. J. Power Sources ACS Appl. Energy Mater.">7</a>, <a href="#adfm202409295-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202409295-bib-0018_R_d93046700e796" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - J. Mater. Chem. A ACS Nano Small">18</a>, <a href="#adfm202409295-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202409295-bib-0022_R_d93046700e796" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - J. Power Sources J. Mater. Chem. A Chem. Eng. J. J. Mater. Chem. A Small">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Differing from XRD results above (metallic Sb is predominant), Sb mainly existed in the form of Sb–S bond detected in Raman, which may be derived from the coordination between Sb, Fe, and S in FF. That is to say, the strong metallicity of Fe could induce the shifting of S element in Sb–Fe–S complex, displaying the obvious characteristic peak of Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S. While the relatively weak bonding of Sb and S tended to exhibit the metallic nature of Sb in XRD. It is worth noting that, the peaks situated at 212.8, 279.7, and 397.6 cm<sup>−1</sup> were assigned to A<sub>g</sub>, E<sub>g</sub>, and T<sub>g</sub> vibration models of Fe–S in Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S, meaning the abundant phase boundaries/defects of FF.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202409295-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202409295-bib-0023_R_d93046700e814" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - J. Mater. Chem. A Green Energy Environ. J Energy Chem Phys. Chem. Chem. Phys. Inorg. Chem. Front. J. Mater. Sci.: Mater. Electron.">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In addition, for FD and FF, the typical peaks at 1352.8 cm<sup>−1</sup> and 1593.7 cm<sup>−1</sup> were related to the sp<sup>2</sup> hybridized disordered carbon (D band) and graphitic carbon (G band), respectively, mainly originating from vacuum pyrolysis and carbonization of the precursors.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202409295-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202409295-bib-0024_R_d93046700e826" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - J. Mater. Chem. A Adv. Funct. Mater. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Electrochim. Acta">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The calculated I<sub>D</sub>/I<sub>G</sub> values for FD and FF were ≈0.79 and 0.77, respectively, revealing that the graphitic carbon was predominant. The low I<sub>D</sub>/I<sub>G</sub> values indicated the high graphitization degree of carbon layers, which was conducive to the improvement of electrical conductivity and ion/e<sup>−</sup> diffusion kinetics.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202409295-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202409295-bib-0022_R_d93046700e842" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - J. Power Sources J. Mater. Chem. A Chem. Eng. J. J. Mater. Chem. A Small">22</a>, <a href="#adfm202409295-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202409295-bib-0025_R_d93046700e842" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - J. Alloys Compd. J. Mater. Chem. A Dalton Trans.">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The electrical conductivity of as-obtained samples was exhibited in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202409295-fig-0002">2d</a>. Notably, FF displayed considerable conductivity (15.90 × 10<sup>−1</sup> S cm<sup>−1</sup>), much higher than that of FP (0.03 × 10<sup>−1</sup> S cm<sup>−1</sup>) and FD (0.70 × 10<sup>−1</sup> S cm<sup>−1</sup>), further demonstrating the unique advantages of bimetallic phases introduction and graphitic carbon capsulation. Pore size distribution and specific surface area displayed a crucial influence on energy storage abilities. Hence, N<sub>2</sub> adsorption-desorption isotherms of all samples were shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202409295-fig-0002">2e</a>, the typical IV-type adsorption-desorption isotherms with obvious hysteresis loop of H3 for FD and FF were noted, indicating their rich mesoporous structures.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202409295-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202409295-bib-0007_R_d93046700e869" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Nano-Micro Lett. Nano Energy Solid State Commun. J. Power Sources ACS Appl. Energy Mater.">7</a>, <a href="#adfm202409295-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202409295-bib-0021_R_d93046700e869" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Chem. Eng. J. Small">21</a>, <a href="#adfm202409295-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202409295-bib-0026_R_d93046700e869" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - J. Colloid Interface Sci.">26</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Compared to FD and FP, the larger specific surface area of FF was found (in order of FP (0.23 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>−1</sup>) &lt; FD (7.61 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>−1</sup>) &lt; FF (11.42 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>−1</sup>)), mainly ascribed to the incorporation of bimetallic phases and graphitic carbon, inducing the formation of abundant phase boundaries and defects/pores. Moreover, FD and FF exhibited typical mesoporous structures with pore size distribution of 2–50&nbsp;nm (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3c</a>, Supporting Information), associating well with the above results. In general, the enhanced surface area and abundant mesoporous distribution were beneficial for the infiltration of electrolytes, the diffusion of Na<sup>+</sup>/e<sup>−</sup> and exposure of sufficient active sites, thus effectively broadening energy distribution and suppressing irreversible two-phase separation. As-known, the content of the metal phase served as key role in strengthening electrochemical kinetics and reversibility.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202409295-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202409295-bib-0021_R_d93046700e895" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Chem. Eng. J. Small">21</a>, <a href="#adfm202409295-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202409295-bib-0027_R_d93046700e895" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Carbon Energy">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Thus, the metallic Sb content for as-prepared samples was estimated by the thermogravimetric analysis (TGA) in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202409295-fig-0002">2f</a>. The weight loss of FP, FD, and FF were 34.19%, 36.44%, and 34.46%, respectively, mainly derived from the oxidation of bimetallic sulfide composites.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202409295-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202409295-bib-0002_R_d93046700e903" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Nano Energy Adv. Energy Mater. Nano Res. Adv. Funct. Mater. J. Mater. Chem. A">2</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> From XRD analysis of the residual products in Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3b</a> (Supporting Information), it could be confirmed that they have been converted into Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>, FeSbO<sub>4</sub>/Sb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4,</sub> and FeSbO<sub>4</sub>, respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202409295-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202409295-bib-0025_R_d93046700e925" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - J. Alloys Compd. J. Mater. Chem. A Dalton Trans.">25</a>, <a href="#adfm202409295-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202409295-bib-0028_R_d93046700e925" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Mater. Lett. J. Mater. Chem. A New J. Chem.">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Notably, the only product FeSbO<sub>4</sub> was detected, further manifesting the coordination and bonding of Sb, Fe, and S in FF. Based on the equation (W<sub>Sb</sub>% = (1-34.46%) * M<sub>Sb</sub>/M<sub>FeSbO4</sub>), the content of metallic Sb for FF was calculated ≈33.01%, higher than that of FD (&lt; 32.01%) and FP (1.30%, W<sub>Sb</sub>% = 1–65.81% * 2M<sub>FeS2</sub>/M<sub>Fe2O3</sub>). It could be found that the rational Sb-phase content was beneficial for electrical conductivity improvement and sulfur phase tight adsorption/anchoring. To further investigate their functional groups of as-obtained samples, FTIR spectrum was displayed in Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3d</a> (Supporting Information). The absorption peaks at 3450 and 1124 cm<sup>−1</sup> were associated with typical O–H stretching vibration and asymmetric S–O stretching, respectively. It is worth noting that the peaks at 500–700 cm<sup>−1</sup> correspond to Fe–S–Sb bond vibration, revealing the formation of electric/chemical coupling in Fe/Sb heterojunction.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202409295-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202409295-bib-0023_R_d93046700e953" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - J. Mater. Chem. A Green Energy Environ. J Energy Chem Phys. Chem. Chem. Phys. Inorg. Chem. Front. J. Mater. Sci.: Mater. Electron.">23</a>, <a href="#adfm202409295-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202409295-bib-0029_R_d93046700e953" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Chem. Eng. J. J. Alloys Compd. RSC Adv. J. Alloys Compd. Chemosphere Electrochim. Acta">29</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Besides, for FD and FF, the representative carbon bands of C═C (1500-1750 cm<sup>−1</sup>) and C–S–Sb (750-1000 cm<sup>−1</sup>) were noted, disclosing that a similar chemical evolution occurs at Sb/C heterointerface during in situ carbonization, contributing to further strengthen active phase controlling and sulfur phase trapping/anchoring.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202409295-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202409295-bib-0002_R_d93046700e962" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Nano Energy Adv. Energy Mater. Nano Res. Adv. Funct. Mater. J. Mater. Chem. A">2</a>, <a href="#adfm202409295-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202409295-bib-0004_R_d93046700e962" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Chem. Mater. Adv. Funct. Mater. Energy Storage Mater. Adv. Mater. Interfaces">4</a>, <a href="#adfm202409295-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202409295-bib-0007_R_d93046700e962" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Nano-Micro Lett. Nano Energy Solid State Commun. J. Power Sources ACS Appl. Energy Mater.">7</a>, <a href="#adfm202409295-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202409295-bib-0030_R_d93046700e962" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Electrochim. Acta Adv. Mater.">30</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In summary, FF with double-layers heterojunctions (Fe/Sb and Sb/C heterojunction) and interfacial C–S–Sb/Sb–S–Fe bonds -were successfully prepared, which was expected to induce interesting interface electric/chemical coupling effect.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202409295-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/e4648d39-00b2-461d-8922-d2b1cd59735a/adfm202409295-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/e4648d39-00b2-461d-8922-d2b1cd59735a/adfm202409295-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/e9487d95-e2f9-4607-870f-2ada77b442cc/adfm202409295-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/e4648d39-00b2-461d-8922-d2b1cd59735a/adfm202409295-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202409295-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202409295" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">The physicochemical properties of as-resulted samples: a) the simple mechanism of energy-storage behaviors, b) XRD patterns of FF, c) Raman spectrum, d) electrical conductivity, e) TG curves, f) specific surface area.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>In addition, X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) was employed to explore the surface composition and chemical states of as-prepared samples as shown in <b>Figures</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202409295-fig-0003">3</a> and&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a> (Supporting Information). First, through their full survey scan spectrum analysis (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4a–c</a>, Supporting Information), the main characteristic peaks of Fe2p, S2p, Sb3d, and C1s were detected, revealing the formation of Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S@Sb (matching well with XRD results above). Fe2p high-resolution spectrums of FP, FD, and FF were displayed in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202409295-fig-0003">3a–c</a>, and the peaks at 707.1/719.9&nbsp;eV and 708.9/722.1&nbsp;eV were assigned to Fe2p<sub>3/2</sub> and Fe2p<sub>1/2</sub> of FeS<sub>2</sub>/FeS, respectively, indicating that Fe<sup>2+</sup> was dominant in FP.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202409295-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202409295-bib-0014_R_d93046700e1015" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Small">14</a>, <a href="#adfm202409295-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202409295-bib-0019_R_d93046700e1015" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Mater. Lett.">19</a>, <a href="#adfm202409295-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202409295-bib-0031_R_d93046700e1015" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Chem. Eng. J.">31</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Compared to FP, the characteristic peaks indexed to Fe<sup>0</sup> (707.7/720.5&nbsp;eV) and Fe<sup>3+</sup> (712.8/726.6&nbsp;eV) have been also detected in FF, revealing its rich Fe valence state. Meanwhile, the relatively weak and broad Fe2p spectrum of FD was noted, perhaps attributed to its thicker carbon coating.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202409295-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202409295-bib-0032_R_d93046700e1024" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - ACS Nano">32</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> For S2p spectrum in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202409295-fig-0003">3d–f</a>, the peaks at 162.4&nbsp;eV/163.6&nbsp;eV and 164.8/166.1&nbsp;eV were noted, corresponding to S<sub>2</sub><sup>2−</sup> and S<sub>n</sub><sup>2-</sup> (S2p<sub>3/2</sub>/S2p<sub>1/2</sub>), respectively, While the weaker peaks at 168.1/169.4&nbsp;eV were associated with SO<sub>x</sub>, perhaps ascribed to the slight oxidation of as-obtained samples.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202409295-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202409295-bib-0010_R_d93046700e1046" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - ACS Energy Lett.">10</a>, <a href="#adfm202409295-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202409295-bib-0023_R_d93046700e1046" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - J. Mater. Chem. A Green Energy Environ. J Energy Chem Phys. Chem. Chem. Phys. Inorg. Chem. Front. J. Mater. Sci.: Mater. Electron.">23</a>, <a href="#adfm202409295-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202409295-bib-0026_R_d93046700e1046" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - J. Colloid Interface Sci.">26</a>, <a href="#adfm202409295-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202409295-bib-0033_R_d93046700e1046" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Nano Res. J. Mater. Res.">33</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In addition, the peak intensity of S<sub>n</sub><sup>2−</sup> for FD and FF was noticed, much higher than that of FP, revealing their abundant S species and heterointerface reaction. Interestingly, compared to that of FP, the binding energy shifting (Fe<sup>2+</sup>: blue shift, S<sub>n</sub><sup>2−</sup>: right shift) and polyvalent forms coexisting (0, +3, −2) of Fe2p and S2p were observed in FD and FF, mainly originated from the strong electronic coupling effect between Fe, S and Sb element, thereby leading to the migration of electron cloud density.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202409295-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202409295-bib-0006_R_d93046700e1060" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Adv. Mater. Energy Environ. Sci. Energy Environ. Sci. Adv. Energy Mater. Adv. Funct. Mater.">6</a>, <a href="#adfm202409295-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202409295-bib-0015_R_d93046700e1060" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - ACS Nano">15</a>, <a href="#adfm202409295-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202409295-bib-0016_R_d93046700e1060" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Energy Storage Mater. Adv. Funct. Mater. Nano-Micro Lett. Sci. China: Chem. ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces J. Mater. Chem. A Energy Storage Mater. Energy Storage Mater.">16</a>, <a href="#adfm202409295-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202409295-bib-0034_R_d93046700e1060" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Chem. Eng. J.">34</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> That is to say, profiting from the introduction of bimetallic phases, Fe/Sb heterojunction, and built-in electric field were formed, accompanied by powerful coupling effect and rapid charge transfer between Fe–S and Sb–S bonds, thus bringing about the considerable ion/e<sup>−</sup> diffusion kinetics and storage ability. From the Sb high-resolution spectrum of as-resulted samples in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202409295-fig-0003">3g–i</a>, three pairs of peaks situated at 528.7/538.6&nbsp;eV, 529.9/539.5&nbsp;eV and 530.6/540.3&nbsp;eV (Sb3d<sub>5/2</sub>/Sb3d<sub>3/2</sub>) could be found, assigned to Sb<sup>0</sup>, Sb<sup>3+</sup> (Sb<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>) and Sb<sup>5+</sup> (Sb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>), respectively. Additionally, the overlapping peak of Sb3d5/2 at 531.8&nbsp;eV was related to SO<sub>x</sub>/C═O/C–O of O1s, mainly attributed to the partial oxidization of materials.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202409295-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202409295-bib-0016_R_d93046700e1092" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Energy Storage Mater. Adv. Funct. Mater. Nano-Micro Lett. Sci. China: Chem. ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces J. Mater. Chem. A Energy Storage Mater. Energy Storage Mater.">16</a>, <a href="#adfm202409295-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202409295-bib-0024_R_d93046700e1092" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - J. Mater. Chem. A Adv. Funct. Mater. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Electrochim. Acta">24</a>, <a href="#adfm202409295-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202409295-bib-0035_R_d93046700e1092" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - Adv. Funct. Mater. Adv. Energy Mater.">35</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Compared to FP, the enhanced Sb<sup>0</sup>/Sb<sup>3+</sup> peak intensity of FD and FF were found, indicating the formation of rich metallic Sb<sup>0</sup> and Sb–S bonds. The large peak area of Sb<sup>0</sup>/Sb<sup>3+</sup> (Sb3d<sub>5/2</sub>/Sb3d<sub>3/2</sub>) meant the high content of obtained metallic Sb and Sb–S, significantly facilitating electrical conductivity enhancing and sulfur phase trapping/anchoring. Moreover, the C1s spectrum of FD and FF were shown in Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4d,e</a> (Supporting Information), three peaks at 284.8, 286.5, and 289.9&nbsp;eV were associated with C–C/C═C, C–S–Sb and O–C═O, respectively, further demonstrating the formation of Sb/C heterointerface (matching well with FTIR results above).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202409295-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202409295-bib-0004_R_d93046700e1116" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Chem. Mater. Adv. Funct. Mater. Energy Storage Mater. Adv. Mater. Interfaces">4</a>, <a href="#adfm202409295-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202409295-bib-0007_R_d93046700e1116" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Nano-Micro Lett. Nano Energy Solid State Commun. J. Power Sources ACS Appl. Energy Mater.">7</a>, <a href="#adfm202409295-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202409295-bib-0016_R_d93046700e1116" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Energy Storage Mater. Adv. Funct. Mater. Nano-Micro Lett. Sci. China: Chem. ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces J. Mater. Chem. A Energy Storage Mater. Energy Storage Mater.">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Based on the discussions above, FF with Fe/Sb/C double-layers heterojunctions and interfacial C–S–Sb/Sb–S–Fe bonds was rationally tailored, accompanied by stronger electronic coupling effect and faster ion/charge transport. It was beneficial for improving electrical conductivity and alleviating M/S phase separation/coarsening, resulting in excellent electrochemical reaction kinetics and reversibility.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202409295-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/4798bd05-39c4-4818-aa75-179b2eb2ccf8/adfm202409295-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/4798bd05-39c4-4818-aa75-179b2eb2ccf8/adfm202409295-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/dad5b31d-eaf5-4497-b6cd-819484048854/adfm202409295-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/4798bd05-39c4-4818-aa75-179b2eb2ccf8/adfm202409295-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202409295-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202409295" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">The analysis of elements valence: high-resolution spectrums of a) Fe2p, d) S2p, and g) Sb3d of FP, b) Fe2p, e) S2p, and h) Sb3d of FD, c) Fe2p, f) S2p and i) Sb3d of FF.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <div class="paragraph-element">As everyone knows, morphology and crystalline structure are always closely related to capacity abilities. Thus, scanning electron microscopy (SEM) and transmission electron microscopy (TEM) results of as-prepared samples were performed in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202409295-fig-0004">4</a> and Figures&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a> and&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a> (Supporting Information). From Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5a1,a2</a> (Supporting Information) and Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202409295-fig-0004">4A1,B1</a>, it could be observed that FP/FD display the blocky and lamellar aggregation structure, accompanied by large particle size of 5–10&nbsp;µm, perhaps aggravating structural stress enlarging and materials pulverization. This severe stacking and caking were mainly ascribed to incomplete reaction and uneven growth during vacuum gasification. Besides, the smooth surface revealed high crystallinity and energy barrier, leading to active sites decreasing, energy distribution shrinking, and unsatisfactory pseudocapacitive contributions.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202409295-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202409295-bib-0036_R_d93046700e1161" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - Adv. Energy Mater.">36</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In contrast, for FF, benefitting from the unique sandwich structure of ferrocene and in situ gasification, the nanosheets-assembled flower-like microstructures with diameters of 1 um were shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202409295-fig-0004">4C1</a> and Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5a3</a> (Supporting Information). Interestingly, this hierarchical structure composed of curled and overlapped nanosheets displayed good uniformity and stability, which was expected to bring about considerable energy-storage ability.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202409295-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202409295-bib-0037_R_d93046700e1173" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - J. Power Sources J. Phys. Chem. C">37</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Note that, Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5b1–b4,c1–c5,d1–d5</a> (Supporting Information) displayed the elemental mapping images. Compared to the element distribution of FP and FD, that of FF was more uniform, contributing to the tight pinning and adsorption of bimetallic phases toward the sulfur phase, thus inducing excellent electrochemical reaction kinetics and reversibility. For further exploring the internal structure, TEM images of as-prepared samples were performed in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202409295-fig-0004">4A2–C2</a> and Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6a1–c1</a> (Supporting Information). FP, FD, and FF displayed the blocky/rodlike, lamellar/blocky, and uniform lamellar structure, respectively, mainly ascribed to the different reaction mechanisms during vacuum gasification, coinciding well with the SEM results above. In high-resolution transmission electron microscopy (HRTEM) of FP and FD (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202409295-fig-0004">4A3,B3</a>; Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6a2,b2</a>, Supporting Information), the dispersed lattice fringes distance was ≈0.27/0.19 and 0.18/0.22&nbsp;nm, indexing to Sb<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>/FeS<sub>2</sub> (221)/(220) and FeSb<sub>2</sub>S<sub>4</sub> (521)/(411) crystal planes, respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202409295-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202409295-bib-0014_R_d93046700e1205" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Small">14</a>, <a href="#adfm202409295-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202409295-bib-0015_R_d93046700e1205" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - ACS Nano">15</a>, <a href="#adfm202409295-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202409295-bib-0034_R_d93046700e1205" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Chem. Eng. J.">34</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The corresponding selected area electron diffraction (SAED) patterns further showed diffraction rings assigned to their crystal planes (inset of Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202409295-fig-0004">4A3,B3</a>), agreeing well with the above XRD results. Noticed that the uneven lattice distribution perhaps brought about the enlarging of lattice stress, resulting in serious M/S phase separation, Fe/Sb nanoparticles coarsening, and soluble NaPSs shuttling, accompanied by inferior Na-storage abilities. Interestingly, for FF (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202409295-fig-0004">4C3</a>; Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6c2</a>, Supporting Information), the lattice fringes distance of 0.22 and 0.26&nbsp;nm were detected, corresponding well to Sb (104) and Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S (207) crystal planes, respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202409295-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202409295-bib-0016_R_d93046700e1222" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Energy Storage Mater. Adv. Funct. Mater. Nano-Micro Lett. Sci. China: Chem. ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces J. Mater. Chem. A Energy Storage Mater. Energy Storage Mater.">16</a>, <a href="#adfm202409295-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202409295-bib-0018_R_d93046700e1222" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - J. Mater. Chem. A ACS Nano Small">18</a>, <a href="#adfm202409295-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202409295-bib-0024_R_d93046700e1222" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - J. Mater. Chem. A Adv. Funct. Mater. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Electrochim. Acta">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The clear crystal phase interface could be found, strongly verifying the formation of the inner Fe/Sb heterointerface. Meanwhile, this structure was tightly encapsulated into the graphitic matrix with a thickness of 4&nbsp;nm through effective chemical attraction, thus constructing an outer Sb/C heterojunction (C–S–Sb bond). The double-layer heterojunctions could strengthen metal-phase boundary distribution and built-in electric field, inducing a strong electronic coupling effect and abundant active sites, thereby significantly enhancing electrical conductivity and M/S phase separation controlling/adsorption. Furthermore, the interfaces and lattice mismatch (m) could be calculated as follows:<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202409295-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202409295-bib-0038_R_d93046700e1227" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - Nano Energy">38</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup><div class="inline-equation" id="adfm202409295-disp-0001"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
