               <div class="article-section__table-source"></div>
            </div>
            
            <p>To further explore the electrochemical kinetic advantages of double-layer heterojunctions, the galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) was carried out to evaluate Na-ion diffusion behaviors of as-obtained samples as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202409295-fig-0007">7g</a>. From GITT single-step discharge process diagram at 0.80&nbsp;V (inset of Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202409295-fig-0007">7g</a>) and Equation&nbsp;(<a class="equationLink scrollableLink" href="#adfm202409295-disp-0021" title="Link to equation">21</a>), it could be known that τ represents the relaxation time (180&nbsp;min), <i>S</i>, <i>V</i><sub>m</sub>, <i>M</i><sub>B,</sub> and <i>m</i><sub>B</sub> are the surface area (1.13 cm<sup>2</sup>), molar volume, molecular weight and mass of active materials, respectively, Δ<i>E</i><sub>t</sub> is the transient potential change before and after discharge (Δ<i>E</i><sub>t</sub> = <i>E</i><sub>0</sub>- <i>E</i><sub>t</sub>), and Δ<i>E</i><sub>s</sub> is the steady-state voltage by the constant current pulse (Δ<i>E</i><sub>s</sub> = <i>E</i><sub>0</sub>- <i>E</i><sub>s</sub>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202409295-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202409295-bib-0009_R_d93046700e3524" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Small J. Mater. Chem. A Adv. Funct. Mater. Adv. Funct. Mater. Ceram. Int. Adv. Energy Mater.">9</a>, <a href="#adfm202409295-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202409295-bib-0014_R_d93046700e3524" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Small">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Through further calculation, the corresponding D<sub>Na+</sub> of FP, FD, and FF during the sodiation–desodiation process were performed in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202409295-fig-0007">7h</a>. According to previous studies, the diffusion coefficient varied with the charge–discharge process, where the minimum D<sub>Na+</sub> values appeared at each redox plateau, mainly ascribed to the deep diffusion of Na-ion into/from the crystalline framework. The subsequent enhancement of the Na-ion diffusion coefficient (the higher D<sub>Na+</sub>) was derived from the nanocrystalline domains of bimetallic sulfides, obviously shortening the solid-phase diffusion distance.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202409295-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202409295-bib-0013_R_d93046700e3540" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Adv. Energy Mater.">13</a>, <a href="#adfm202409295-bib-0049" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202409295-bib-0049_R_d93046700e3540" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 49 - Energy Storage Mater.">49</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Notably, compared to FD and FP, FF possessed the higher D<sub>Na+</sub>-values at 1.20, 0.63, and 0.26&nbsp;V during the discharging process, corresponding to the Na<sup>+</sup>-intercalation into Fe<sub>1-x</sub>Sb<sub>y</sub>S, the conversion reaction of Na<sub>z</sub>Fe<sub>1-x</sub>Sb<sub>y</sub>S into metallic Fe/Sb and Na<sub>2</sub>S, and the alloying reaction of between Na<sup>+</sup> and metallic Sb, respectively. Similarly, during the charging process, the relatively high D<sub>Na+</sub> located at 0.93 and1.45&nbsp;V were associated with the dealloying/deconversion reaction from Na<sub>3</sub>Sb, metallic Fe/Sb into Na<sub>z</sub>Fe<sub>1-x</sub>Sb<sub>y</sub>S and further deintercalation process. It could be concluded that the accelerated sodiation–desodiation diffusion kinetics was closely related to the high-reversible conversion-alloying reaction induced by double-layers heterojunctions and interfacial C–S–Sb/Sb–S–Fe bonds, coinciding well with CV analysis above. In addition, the average D<sub>Na+</sub>-values of FF were 7.29/7.88 × 10<sup>−12</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup> during the discharging–charging process, much higher than that of FD (4.82/4.98 × 10<sup>−12</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup>) and FP (4.35/4.78 × 10<sup>−12</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup>), further revealing its remarkable ion transport behaviors. In general, the construction of double-layer heterojunctions and interfacial C–S–Sb/Sb–S–Fe bonds played crucial roles in enhancing diffusion kinetics and ion-storage capacity. On the one hand, this interesting structural evolution was helpful for alleviating M/S phase separation, Fe/Sb nanoparticles coarsening, and soluble NaPSs shuttling during sodiation–desodiation process, effectively improving the structural stability and conversion-alloying reaction reversibility. On the other hand, the double-layer heterointerface/phase boundaries and built-in electric field were formed, accompanied by a strong electronic coupling effect and abundant crystal defects/active sites, contributing to the decreasing of interfacial resistance and the enhancement of ion/e<sup>−</sup> diffusion kinetics.</p>
            
            <p>As well-known, the stability/integrity of morphology and crystalline structures were crucial for achieving excellent cycling performance during the high-rate charge–discharge process. The structural evolution of FP, FD, and FF after 100 cycles was investigated through ex situ SEM, SAED, and HRTEM as displayed in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202409295-fig-0008">8</a>. As expected, the obvious pulverization and aggregation of FP and FD were noted in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202409295-fig-0008">8A1-2,B1-2</a>, accompanied by the uneven reassembly of flaky, blocky, and wafer structure, indicating irreversible conversion reactions and unstable structural distribution during continuous sodiation–desodiation process. While the relatively smooth surface and flower-like structures of FF confirmed that the reversibility and stability for metallic Fe/Sb and S phases were strongly enhanced by the synergistic effect of double-layers heterojunctions and interfacial C–S–Sb/Sb–S–Fe bonds (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202409295-fig-0008">8C1</a>). From Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202409295-fig-0008">8C2</a>, it could be deduced that M/S phase separation and Fe/Sb nanoparticles coarsening would be effectively inhibited during repeated charging–discharging process, thereby inducing the stable reassembly of nanosheets and the orientated growth of flower-like microstructures. Moreover, the corresponding elemental mapping images of as-obtained samples are shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202409295-fig-0008">8D–F</a>. the uneven distribution and point agglomeration of Sb (red/orange area), Fe (purple area), and S element (yellow area) for FP and FD was noted, disclosing the separation of active phases (Sb, Fe, and S) and Fe/Sb nanoparticles agglomeration/coarsening, which could well explain their irreversible capacity loss. That of FF was relatively uniform, further demonstrating the great advantages of bimetallic phases and interfacial C–S–Sb/Sb–S–Fe “bridge” bonds in anchoring/controlling the sulfur phase. To further explore their internal structure and phase transformations, the ex situ HRTEM images of FP, FD, and FF at the 100th cycle charged were displayed in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202409295-fig-0008">8A3–C3</a>. As expected, the heterogeneous Fe<sup>0</sup> and Sb<sup>0</sup> nanoparticles distribution were observed (Fe (110)/ Sb (205) crystal planes with lattice spacing of 0.21/0.14&nbsp;nm), revealing the severe irreversible conversion/alloying reaction of FP and partial irreversible process of FD (the regeneration of FeSb<sub>2</sub>S<sub>4</sub>) after cycles. In contrast, FF after cycling was still tightly anchored onto the carbon layer without any obvious agglomeration or pulverization, deducing their effective chemical affinity. More importantly, the lattice fringes with a spacing of 0.22, 0.31, and 0.17&nbsp;nm were assigned to FeS (200), Sb<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> (121), and Sb (202) crystal planes, respectively, indicating the reversible regeneration and homogeneous distribution of bimetallic sulfides FeS/Sb<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>/Sb. In addition, to fully understand the interfacial charge transfer and impedance changes during the electrochemical reaction, in situ electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) of FP, FD, and FF at different charging–discharging voltages was performed in Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S23a–f</a> (Supporting Information). During the discharging process, their charge transfer impedance increased gradually with the decreasing of voltages, corresponding to the phase transformation from the insertion reaction to the conversion/alloying reaction (the sodiation process). While those of the charging process displayed the opposite trend, coinciding with the desodiation process (deconversion/alloying reaction to deinsertion reaction). The obvious resistance increasing/change of FP was noted during charging–discharging process, mainly ascribed to the huge volume expansion induced by nanograin coarsening and phase separation. The high interface impedance would inhibit ion/e<sup>−</sup> fast migration and affected rate abilities. Compared to the resistance change of FP, that of FF and FD only experienced slight fluctuation, indicating that bimetallic Fe/Sb/C heterojunctions and C–S–Sb/Sb–S–Fe bonds are in favor of mitigating volume expansion and enhancing ion/e<sup>−</sup> exchange efficiency. Interestingly, the resistance values of FF were consistently lower than those of FP and FD, further illustrating its property advantages. Generally speaking, the construction of double-layer heterojunctions and interfacial C–S–Sb/Sb–S–Fe “bridge” bonds could effectively alleviate two-phase separation, Fe/Sb nanoparticles coarsening and soluble NaPSs shuttling, thus leading to reversible and stable sodiation–desodiation process for FF. Based on the discussion above, it could be summarized: 1) the fundamental mechanism of capacity fading was dominated by the separation/aggregation of Sb/Fe and S phase, 2) the remarkable structural stability and electrochemical reversibility were mainly attributed to the synergistic effect of double-layers heterojunctions and interfacial C–S–Sb/Sb–S–Fe “bridge” bonds.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202409295-fig-0008"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/b63094d1-7c35-4101-bdf7-81bd82747159/adfm202409295-fig-0008-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/b63094d1-7c35-4101-bdf7-81bd82747159/adfm202409295-fig-0008-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/5024d0f0-348d-42a5-9269-f5d857284ef8/adfm202409295-fig-0008-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/b63094d1-7c35-4101-bdf7-81bd82747159/adfm202409295-fig-0008-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 8<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202409295-fig-0008&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202409295" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">The ex situ results of the as-targeted samples after cycles: SEM, Mapping, and HRTEM images for FP (A1–A3 and D1–D4), for FD (B1–B3 and E1–E5), for FF (C1–C3 and F1–F5).</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>Triggered by the superior ultrafast Na-ion storage capacities of FF, the full cell of FF//NVP with FF anode and commercial Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> cathode were assembled to further evaluate its practical application, in which the N/P was selected as 1.10 after several repeated experiments. The corresponding ion-storage mechanism was proposed in Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S23g</a> (Supporting Information). Interesting structural evolution (Fe/Sb, Sb/C heterojunction, and interfacial C–S–Sb/Sb–S–Fe “bridge” bonds) displayed crucial roles in alleviating M/S phases separation/polarization, obviously lowering the interfacial resistances and strengthening ion/e<sup>−</sup> migration. Herein, FF//NVP delivered the initial discharge capacity of 426.3 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> and a considerable reversible capacity of 333.8 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> with a retention rate of 78.2% after 100 loops at 0.5 A g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S23h</a>, Supporting Information). Moreover, the GCD profiles of different cycles (1st, 10th, 20th, 50th, 70th, and 100th) were shown in Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S23i</a> (Supporting Information), accompanied by the stable sodiation–desodiation platforms and relatively smaller ΔV, further rendering the promising potential of FF as SIB full-cell anodes.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202409295-sec-0070">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202409295-sec-0070-title">2.5 Exploring of Na-ion Storage Abilities at Low-Temperature −5 °C</h3>
            
            <p>Considering the impressive Na-ion storage capabilities of FF at room temperature, its practical application potential at low-temperature (−5 °C) was further evaluated in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202409295-fig-0009">9</a>. From previous reports, the capacity and cycling stability fading was mostly derived from Gibbs free energy (Δ<i>G</i> = Δ<i>H</i> – <i>T</i>Δ<i>S</i>) changing and reaction kinetic coefficients weakening at low temperatures (LT).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202409295-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202409295-bib-0040_R_d93046700e3720" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - J. Mater. Chem. A">40</a>, <a href="#adfm202409295-bib-0050" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202409295-bib-0050_R_d93046700e3720" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 50 - ChemNanoMat Energy Environ. Sci.">50</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> That significantly aggravated the polarization and uncontrolled sodium dendrites growth during sodiation–desodiation process, thus resulting in poor ion/e- diffusion kinetics and conductivity. Fortunately, benefitting from the great advantages of double-layers heterojunctions and interfacial C–S–Sb/Sb–S–Fe “bridge” bonds, the electrochemical reaction kinetic/reversibility could be improved at LT. In Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202409295-fig-0009">9a</a>, the initial charge–discharge capacity for FF was 386.0/481.6 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup> and delivered a reversible capacity of 349.7 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> with a retention rate of 72.6% after 100 loops. Even at 0.5 A g<sup>−1</sup>, its initial discharge capacity could reach up to 321.9 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> and still remained at ≈116.1 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 100 cycles, indicating its remarkable cycling stabilities at LT. Moreover, at various current densities of 0.1, 0.2, 0.5, 0.8, and 1.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>, its average reversible capacities were ≈320.8, 278.0, 187.6, 133.1 and 98.9 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202409295-fig-0009">9d</a>). The reversible capacity of 432.0 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> could be achieved as the current density was restored to 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup>, further disclosing that its superior rate performance was mostly ascribed to the interesting interfacial heterostructure evolution. For investigating the effect of LT on the reaction process in detail, the galvanostatic charge–discharge profiles and corresponding differential capacity versus potential curves (<i>dQ/dV</i>) of FF at 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup> from 2nd to 100th cycles were exhibited in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202409295-fig-0009">9b,e</a>. the overlapping charge–discharge curves and redox peaks were noted, corresponding to the stable sodiation–desodiation process and capacity contribution, accompanied by relatively small ΔV, mainly attributed to its high bimetallic conductivity and reversible conversion-alloying reaction kinetics. Interestingly, from its discharge capacity contribution in three reaction stages at 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup> from the 2nd to 100th loops (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202409295-fig-0009">9f</a>), it could be found that the stable insertion, conversion, and alloying reaction contribution remained ≈40%, 30%, and 30%, respectively, further revealing its excellent stability/reversibility at LT, matching well with the above <i>dQ/dV</i> results. In addition, to explore the electrochemical reaction kinetics and capacitive behaviors of FF, its CV curves from 0.3 to 1.2&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup> were performed in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202409295-fig-0009">9g</a>, where the response currents increased gradually. Compared to CV curves at RT in Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S24</a> (Supporting Information), the peak shifting and peak currents weakening were noted at LT, mainly derived from sluggish reaction kinetics and electrolyte viscosity increasing, thus leading to ion/e- migration rate lowering and voltage polarization aggravating. Based on the linear fitting and further calculation, the values of I<sub>p</sub> versus v<sup>1/2</sup> for Peak1/2 were ≈0.11 and 0.24, the corresponding D<sub>Na+</sub> were 3.74 × 10<sup>−13</sup> and 1.77 × 10<sup>−12</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup>, respectively (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202409295-fig-0009">9h</a>), indicating its superior Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetics at LT. That is to say, owing to the construction of high-conductive double-layers heterointerface and interfacial C–S–Sb/Sb–S–Fe “bridge” bonds, the ion diffusion was less affected by LT. Furthermore, the b values of Peak1/2 were calculated to be 1.2 and 0.6 by the fitting of log(i) versus log(v) in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202409295-fig-0009">9i</a>, disclosing the combined diffusion and pseudocapacitive-controlled mechanism. And as the scan rates increased from 0.3 to 1.2&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup> (0.3, 0.5, 0.7, 0.9, 1.2&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup>), the surface/near-surface capacitive contribution became more prominent in sodium storage, accounting for 59.87%, 59.38%, 63.25%, 71.69% and 84.59% (inset in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202409295-fig-0009">9i</a>), thus resulting in the faster Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion/reaction kinetics. Moreover, from the fitted Nyquist plots and the equivalent circuit model as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202409295-fig-0009">9c</a>, it could be noted that the interface/charge transfer resistance gradually decreased with activation, further demonstrating the superior ion/e<sup>−</sup> transport kinetics achieved by high-conductive bimetallic double-layers heterojunctions and interfacial “bridge” bonds at LT.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202409295-fig-0009"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/8a8c45e5-d8d3-4657-93f3-e05913277e62/adfm202409295-fig-0009-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/8a8c45e5-d8d3-4657-93f3-e05913277e62/adfm202409295-fig-0009-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/a395d85a-197d-40c1-ada4-755d0f9a7084/adfm202409295-fig-0009-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/8a8c45e5-d8d3-4657-93f3-e05913277e62/adfm202409295-fig-0009-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 9<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202409295-fig-0009&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202409295" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">The electrochemical property and kinetic analysis of FF at the low temperature of −5 °C: a) cycling properties at 0.1 and 0.5 A g<sup>−1</sup>, d) the rate cycling performance at stepwise current densities from 0.1 to 1.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>, b,e,f) the sodiation–desodiation profiles, corresponding <i>dQ/dV</i> curves, and the discharge capacity contribution from 2nd –100th at 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup>, g) CV curves at stepwise scan rates from 0.3–1.2&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup>, h) the linear relationship of <i>I</i><sub>p</sub> versus v<sup>1/2</sup> at Peak1/2 and corresponding Na-ion diffusion coefficients, i) the linear relation between log(i) versus log(v) at Peak1/2 (inset of the capacitive contribution at diverse sweep rates), the Nyquist plots, c) the relative fitting circuit and simplified models from 0th–50th.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
         </section>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202409295-sec-0080">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202409295-sec-0080-title">3 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In summary, for enhancing the controlling/anchoring of active M/S phases (Fe/Sb and S), flower-like Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S@Sb@C nanosheets with double-layer heterojunctions were rationally designed with purified stibnite and ferrocene by vacuum calcination and gasification in situ coating manners. In such heterojunctions, bimetallic phases/boundary defects, interfacial C–S–Sb/Sb–S–Fe “bridge” bonds, and built-in electric field were formed, accompanied by strong electronic coupling effect and rich active sites for Na<sup>+</sup> storage, which were beneficial for accelerating ion/charge transfer and reaction kinetics. Moreover, from the “voltage-cutting” and <i>dQ/dV</i> curves analysis of SIBs capacity, it could be further revealed that the fundamental mechanism of capacity fading was dominated by M/S phase separation. Interestingly, the construction of double-layers heterojunctions and interfacial C–S–Sb, Sb–S-Fe bonds could strengthen the tight controlling/anchoring of bimetallic phases toward sulfur phase, contributing to inhibit two-phase separation, Fe/Sb nanoparticles agglomeration/coarsening and soluble NaPSs shuttling, thus improving conversion-alloying reaction reversibility. Assisted by these unique advantages, FF delivered reversible Na-storage capacity of 397.4 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 100 cycles at 5.0 A g<sup>−1</sup> and ultra-fast rate ability of 436.5/334.3 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 10.0/20.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>. Even at low temperature of −5 °C, the reversible capacity was still maintained at 349.7 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup>. Through the detailed electrochemical kinetic analysis and theoretical calculations, the superior Na-ion storage capacity mainly originated from accelerated interfacial ion/e<sup>−</sup> diffusion and surface/near-surface capacitive behaviors. In addition, in/ex situ technologies strongly proved the unique advantages of this interesting interfacial heterostructure evolution, enhancing the stable adsorption/anchoring of two active phases, thereby inducing high-reversible reassembling evolution and sodiation–desodiation process. In general, this work was anticipated to provide significant insight into capacity fading mechanism analysis and a new perspective on designing metal-sulfur anodes for high-rate battery systems.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202409295-sec-0090">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202409295-sec-0090-title">4 Experimental Section</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202409295-sec-0100">
            
            <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202409295-sec-0110">
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202409295-sec-0120">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adfm202409295-sec-0120-title"> The Preparation of Materials</h4>
                  
                  <p>Natural stibnite was obtained from Guangzhou Huadu Ye's Stone Specimen Firm. Citric acid, hydrochloric acid and sodium sulfide nine hydrate were purchased from Sinopharm Chemical Reagent Co., Ltd. Ferrocene (FeC<sub>5</sub>H<sub>5</sub>)<sub>2</sub>), Iron(III) acetylacetonate (Fe(C<sub>5</sub>H<sub>7</sub>O<sub>2</sub>)<sub>3</sub>) and Iron(III) chloride anhydrous (FeCl<sub>3</sub>) were purchased from Shanghai Macklin Biochemical Technology Co., Ltd. All chemicals were purchased from the chemical companies and used without any purification.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202409295-sec-0130">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adfm202409295-sec-0130-title"> Synthesis Process for the As-resulted Samples</h4>
                  
                  <p>Purification of Natural Stibnite Minerals: First, 2&nbsp;g natural Stibnite minerals were ground into micron-sized particles through the planetary ball mill (ND7-4L). Then they were dispersed into 50&nbsp;g 9.0&nbsp;wt% sodium sulfide nine hydrate and stirred for 1.0&nbsp;h to obtain Na<sub>3</sub>SbS<sub>3</sub> solution at 60&nbsp;°C. The supernatant solution was obtained by centrifuging the slurry solution at 12 000&nbsp;rpm min<sup>−1</sup> for 5&nbsp;min. Subsequently, 50&nbsp;mL 0.75&nbsp;C hydrochloric acid solution was slowly injected into the above complex solution to precipitate highly purified Sb<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> nanoparticles with rapid stirring. Finally, after washing with deionized water and absolute ethyl alcohol 4 times, the centrifuged materials were placed in a vacuum drying oven at 60 °C for 12&nbsp;h before use.</p>
                  
                  <p>Preparation of FF, FD, and FP: The synthesis of as-obtained samples with the desired recipes of the precursors was carried out in an evacuated quartz tube sealed. First, 0.15&nbsp;g of Ferrocene and 0.15&nbsp;g of the above-prepared Sb<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> nanoparticles were put into the agate mortar, ground for 0.5&nbsp;h, and mixed uniformly. Then, the mixed materials were poured into a quartz glass tube with an inner diameter of 15&nbsp;mm (ID 15&nbsp;mm). After that, the quartz tube was vacuumed and sealed at a high temperature by a vacuum tube sealing machine (MRVS1002). Subsequently, the tube with reactants at the bottom was placed in the heating zone of the tube furnace and sintered at 600 °C for 2&nbsp;h with a heating rate of 2 °C min<sup>−1</sup>. Through completion of the reaction and natural cooling to room temperature, the quartz tube was broken by a diamond cutter. The finally collected sample was termed Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S@Sb@C (FF for short). For comparison, Fe<sub>x</sub>Sb<sub>y</sub>S<sub>z</sub>@C and FeS<sub>2</sub>@Sb prepared by replacing Ferrocene with Iron(III) acetylacetonate and Iron(III) chloride anhydrous under similar conditions were termed FD and FP, respectively. Notably, for FP, the intermediate products through vacuum calcination were added into 50&nbsp;g Citric acid solution (17.0 wt%) to remove harmful SbCl<sub>3</sub> generated during the sintering reaction. The final materials were dried in a vacuum drying oven at 60&nbsp;°C.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202409295-sec-0140">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adfm202409295-sec-0140-title"> Characterization of the Materials</h4>
                  
                  <p>XRD (Rigaku D/max 2550 VB, Japan) and Raman spectra (Jobin-Yvon Lab RAM HR-800) were employed to explore the phase and crystalline structures. Electrical conductivity, the content of antimony/carbon, specific surface area, and particle size distribution were determined by the four-probe method (ST2722-SD powder resistivity tester), thermogravimetric analysis (TGA, NETZSCH STA449F3) and Brunauer-Emmett-Teller (BET, ASAP2460, N2-adsoprtion–desorption) analysis. The valence states of elements and functional groups on the sample surface were characterized by X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS, Thermo Fisher Scientific K-Alpha+) and FT-IR (Bruker, Vertex FTIR spectrophotometer). And, the surface morphology and internal structure were explored by field emission scanning electron microscopy (SEM, TESCAN MIRA3) and field emission transmission electron microscope (TEM, Talos F200X) equipped with energy dispersive X-Ray spectrometer (EDS).</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202409295-sec-0150">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adfm202409295-sec-0150-title"> Electrochemical Properties</h4>
                  
                  <p>The sodium storage behaviors of the as-prepared samples were measured by assembling CR2016-type half-cells in a glove box filled with high-purity Ar. First, the work electrodes were composed of 70 wt% active materials, 15 wt% conductive agent (Super P), and 15&nbsp;wt% binders (carboxymethyl cellulose). In general, they were uniformly dispersed in deionized water for 6&nbsp;h and then evenly painted on the current collector (copper foil). After being heated at 70&nbsp;°C for 6&nbsp;h under vacuum, the working electrode was tailored into round pieces in a diameter of 1.2&nbsp;cm with a loading weight of ≈1.0&nbsp;mg&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup>. Finally, using metallic sodium and Whatman glass fiber (GF/A) as counter electrode and separator, 1.0&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaClO<sub>4</sub> in ethylene carbonate (EC) and diethyl carbonate (DEC) in the volume ratio of 1:1 with 5.0&nbsp;wt% fluoroethylene carbonate (FEC) as the electrolyte, which was assembled into sodium-ion half-cell. The galvanostatic charge–discharge, cycling stability, rate capability, and Galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) were tested in LAND battery testing system (CT 2001A) in the potential range of 0.01–3.00&nbsp;V (vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na). The cyclic voltammetry (CV) with different scan rates and electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) at 0.01&nbsp;Hz-100&nbsp;kHz were measured on an electrochemical workstation (Multi Autolab M204). In addition, the electrochemical performance of batteries at low temperatures was tested by the high and low-temperature test box (BTC-405). Moreover, the full SIBs of FF//NVP were assembled with an N/P ratio of 1.10 after several repeated experiments.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202409295-sec-0160">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adfm202409295-sec-0160-title"> Computational Methods</h4>
                  
                  <p>The present first-principle DFT calculations were performed by Vienna Ab initio Simulation Package (VASP) with the projector augmented wave (PAW) method.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202409295-bib-0051" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202409295-bib-0051_R_d93046700e3992" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 51 - Comput. Mater. Sci Phys. Rev. B">51</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The exchange-functional was treated using the generalized gradient approximation (GGA) of Perdew–Burke–Ernzerhof (PBE) functional.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202409295-bib-0052" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202409295-bib-0052_R_d93046700e3997" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 52 - Phys. Rev. B">52</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The energy cutoff for the plane wave basis expansion was set to 420&nbsp;eV and the force on each atom &lt;0.02&nbsp;eV Å<sup>−1</sup> was set for the convergence criterion of geometry relaxation. 15 Å vacuum was added along the z direction in order to avoid the interaction between periodic structures. The Brillouin zone integration was sampled by the grid of 2 × 3 × 1. The self-consistent calculations apply a convergence energy threshold of 10<sup>−5</sup>&nbsp;eV. The DFT-D3 method was employed to consider the van der Waals interaction.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202409295-bib-0053" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202409295-bib-0053_R_d93046700e4007" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 53 - J. Chem. Phys.">53</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> All structure visualizations were performed with the VESTA program.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202409295-bib-0054" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202409295-bib-0054_R_d93046700e4012" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 54 - J. Appl. Crystallogr.">54</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The 2D charge density on the (010) crystal plane was plotted, and the iso-surface level was set to 0.28.</p>
                  
                  <div class="paragraph-element">The sodium-ion/sodium sulfide adsorption energy (<i>E</i><sub>ads-Na</sub>/<i>E</i><sub>ads-Na2S</sub>) was calculated by the following equation:

                     <div class="inline-equation" id="adfm202409295-disp-0022"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                           <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/2c3937b0-6477-4f30-90bd-498c651c77ba/adfm202409295-math-0022.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:1616301X:media:adfm202409295:adfm202409295-math-0022" display="block" location="graphic/adfm202409295-math-0022.png">
                              <semantics>

                                          </msub>
                                          <mi>S</mi>
                                       </mrow>
                                    </msub>
                                    <mo linebreak="badbreak">=</mo>
                                    <msub>
                                       <mi>E</mi>
                                       <mrow>
                                          <mi>t</mi>

                                          <mi>l</mi>
                                       </mrow>

                                    <mo linebreak="goodbreak">−</mo>
                                    <msub>
                                       <mi>E</mi>

                                          <mi>r</mi>
                                          <mi>a</mi>

                                          <mi>e</mi>
                                       </mrow>
                                    </msub>
                                    <mo linebreak="goodbreak">−</mo>
                                    <msub>
                                       <mi>E</mi>

                              </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(22)</span></div>where <i>E</i><sub>total</sub>, <i>E</i><sub>substrate</sub>, and <i>E</i><sub>Na</sub>/<i>E</i><sub>Na2S</sub> represented the total energy of the substrate with one Na<sup>+</sup>/Na<sub>2</sub>S, the energy of the substrate, and the energy of one Na<sup>+</sup> /Na<sub>2</sub>S, respectively.</div>
                  </section>
               </section>
            </section>
